<p><i><b>Allosaurus</b></i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɔːr/ 'or' in 'born'">ɔːr</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>) is a <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> of large <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">theropod</a> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaur</a> that lived 155 to 150 million years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Jurassic" title="Late Jurassic">late Jurassic</a> <a href="/wiki/Period_(geology)" title="Period (geology)">period</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kimmeridgian" title="Kimmeridgian">Kimmeridgian</a> to early <a href="/wiki/Tithonian" title="Tithonian">Tithonian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turner1999_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-turner1999-3">[3]</a></sup>). The name "<i>Allosaurus"</i> means "different lizard". It is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ἄλλος</span>/<i>allos</i> ("different, other") and <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">σαῦρος</span>/<i>sauros</i> ("lizard / generic reptile"). The first fossil remains that can definitely be ascribed to this genus were described in 1877 by paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a>, and it became known as <i><a href="/wiki/Antrodemus" title="Antrodemus">Antrodemus</a></i>. As one of the first well-known theropod dinosaurs, it has long attracted attention outside of <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontological</a> circles. Indeed, it has been a top feature in several <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">films</a> and <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentaries</a> about prehistoric life.</p>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> was a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biped" title="Biped">bipedal</a> <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predator</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> was large and equipped with dozens of sharp, serrated <a href="/wiki/Tooth" title="Tooth">teeth</a>. It averaged 8.5 m (28 ft) in length, though fragmentary remains suggest it could have reached over 12 m (39 ft). Relative to the large and powerful hindlimbs, its three-fingered forelimbs were small, and the body was balanced by a long and heavily muscled tail. It is classified as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allosaurid" title="Allosaurid">allosaurid</a>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">carnosaurian</a> theropod dinosaur. The genus has a complicated <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>, and includes an uncertain number of valid <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, the best known of which is <i>A. fragilis</i>. The bulk of <i>Allosaurus</i> remains have come from <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a>, with material also known from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. It was known for over half of the 20th century as <i><a href="/wiki/Antrodemus" title="Antrodemus">Antrodemus</a></i>, but study of the copious remains from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Dinosaur_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry</a> brought the name "<i>Allosaurus"</i> back to prominence, and established it as one of the best-known dinosaurs.</p>
<p>As the most abundant large predator in the <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a>, <i>Allosaurus</i> was at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, probably preying on contemporaneous large herbivorous dinosaurs and perhaps even other predators. Potential prey included <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">ornithopods</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stegosaurid" title="Stegosaurid">stegosaurids</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropod" title="Sauropod">sauropods</a>. Some paleontologists interpret <i>Allosaurus</i> as having had cooperative <a href="/wiki/Social_behavior" title="Social behavior">social behavior</a>, and hunting in packs, while others believe individuals may have been aggressive toward each other, and that congregations of this genus are the result of lone individuals feeding on the same carcasses. It may have attacked large prey by ambush, using its upper jaw like a <a href="/wiki/Hatchet" title="Hatchet">hatchet</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="1024" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg/220px-Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg/330px-Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg/440px-Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_size_comparison.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The size range of <i>Allosaurus</i> and possible synonym <i>Epanterias</i> (largest), compared with a human.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> was a typical large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">theropod</a>, having a massive <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> on a short <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">neck</a>, a long <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> and reduced forelimbs. <i>Allosaurus fragilis</i>, the best-known species, had an average length of 8.5 m (28 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG97_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-DFG97-4">[4]</a></sup> with the largest definitive <i>Allosaurus</i> specimen (<a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">AMNH</a> 680) estimated at 9.7 meters (32 feet) long,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMDML03_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMDML03-5">[5]</a></sup> and an estimated weight of 2.3 metric tons (2.5 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMDML03_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-MMDML03-5">[5]</a></sup> In his 1976 <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a> on <i>Allosaurus</i>, James Madsen mentioned a range of bone sizes which he interpreted to show a maximum length of 12 to 13 m (39 to 43 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> As with dinosaurs in general, weight estimates are debatable, and since 1980 have ranged between 1,500 kilograms (3,300 pounds), 1,000 to 4,000 kg (2,200 to 8,800 lb), and 1,010 kilograms (2,230 pounds) for <a href="/wiki/Mode_(statistics)" title="Mode (statistics)">modal</a> adult weight (not maximum).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRF03_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRF03-7">[7]</a></sup> John Foster, a specialist on the Morrison Formation, suggests that 1,000 kg (2,200 lb) is reasonable for large adults of <i>A. fragilis</i>, but that 700 kg (1,500 lb) is a closer estimate for individuals represented by the average-sized <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">thigh bones</a> he has measured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF07_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-JF07-8">[8]</a></sup> Using the subadult specimen nicknamed "Big Al", researchers using computer modelling arrived at a best estimate of 1,500 kilograms (3,300 lb) for the individual, but by varying parameters they found a range from approximately 1,400 kilograms (3,100 lb) to approximately 2,000 kilograms (4,400 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KTBetal09_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-KTBetal09-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specimens_of_Allosaurus" title="Specimens of Allosaurus">gigantic specimens</a> have been attributed to <i>Allosaurus</i>, but may in fact belong to other genera. The closely related genus <i><a href="/wiki/Saurophaganax" title="Saurophaganax">Saurophaganax</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Sam_Noble_Oklahoma_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Sam Noble Oklahoma Museum of Natural History">OMNH</a> 1708) reached perhaps 10.9 m (36 ft) in length,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMDML03_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-MMDML03-5">[5]</a></sup> and its single species has sometimes been included in the genus <i>Allosaurus</i> as <i>Allosaurus maximus</i>, though recent studies support it as a separate genus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00-10">[10]</a></sup> Another potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specimens_of_Allosaurus" title="Specimens of Allosaurus">specimen of <i>Allosaurus</i></a>, once assigned to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Epanterias" title="Epanterias">Epanterias</a></i> (AMNH 5767), may have measured 12.1 meters (40 feet) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMDML03_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-MMDML03-5">[5]</a></sup> A more recent discovery is a partial skeleton from the Peterson Quarry in Morrison rocks of <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>; this large allosaurid may be another individual of <i>Saurophaganax</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF07b_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-JF07b-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skull">Skull</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Skull">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2781" data-file-width="3153" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_skull_Science_World.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skull, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telus_World_of_Science,_Vancouver" title="Telus World of Science, Vancouver">Telus World of Science, Vancouver</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The skull and teeth of <i>Allosaurus</i> were modestly proportioned for a theropod of its size. Paleontologist <a href="/wiki/Gregory_S._Paul" title="Gregory S. Paul">Gregory S. Paul</a> gives a length of 845 mm (33.3 in) for a skull belonging to an individual he estimates at 7.9 m (26 ft) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> Each <a href="/wiki/Premaxilla" title="Premaxilla">premaxilla</a> (the bones that formed the tip of the snout), held five teeth with D-shaped cross-sections, and each <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">maxilla</a> (the main tooth-bearing bones in the upper jaw) had between 14 and 17 teeth; the number of teeth does not exactly correspond to the size of the bone. Each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dentary" title="Dentary">dentary</a> (the tooth-bearing bone of the lower jaw) had between 14 and 17 teeth, with an average count of 16. The teeth became shorter, narrower, and more curved toward the back of the skull. All of the teeth had saw-like edges. They were shed easily, and were replaced continually, making them common fossils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skull had a pair of <a href="/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)" title="Horn (anatomy)">horns</a> above and in front of the eyes. These horns were composed of extensions of the <a href="/wiki/Lacrimal_bone" title="Lacrimal bone">lacrimal bones</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> and varied in shape and size. There were also lower paired ridges running along the top edges of the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">nasal bones</a> that led into the horns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> The horns were probably covered in a <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> sheath and may have had a variety of functions, including acting as sunshades for the eye,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> being used for display, and being used in combat against other members of the same species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REM77_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-REM77-13">[13]</a></sup> (although they were fragile).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> There was a ridge along the back of the skull roof for muscle attachment, as is also seen in <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inside the lacrimal bones were depressions that may have held <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">glands</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Salt_gland" title="Salt gland">salt glands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBN85_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-DBN85-14">[14]</a></sup> Within the maxillae were <a href="/wiki/Maxillary_sinus" title="Maxillary sinus">sinuses</a> that were better developed than those of more <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> theropods such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratosaurus" title="Ceratosaurus">Ceratosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Marshosaurus" title="Marshosaurus">Marshosaurus</a></i>; they may have been related to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sense_of_smell" title="Sense of smell">sense of smell</a>, perhaps holding something like <a href="/wiki/Vomeronasal_organ" title="Vomeronasal organ">Jacobson's organ</a>. The roof of the braincase was thin, perhaps to improve <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulation</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> The skull and lower jaws had joints that permitted motion within these units. In the lower jaws, the bones of the front and back halves loosely articulated, permitting the jaws to bow outward and increasing the animal's gape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88e_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88e-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Braincase" title="Braincase">braincase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frontal_bone" title="Frontal bone">frontals</a> may also have had a joint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postcranial_skeleton">Postcranial skeleton</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Postcranial skeleton">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Revised.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2093" data-file-width="4570" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Allosaurus_Revised.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_Revised.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Allosaurus_Revised.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_Revised.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Allosaurus_Revised.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_Revised.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Revised.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Life restoration of <i>A. fragilis</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> had nine <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">vertebrae</a> in the neck, 14 in the back, and five in the <a href="/wiki/Sacrum" title="Sacrum">sacrum</a> supporting the hips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The number of tail vertebrae is unknown and varied with individual size; James Madsen estimated about 50,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> while Gregory S. Paul considered that to be too many and suggested 45 or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> There were hollow spaces in the neck and <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">anterior</a> back vertebrae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> Such spaces, which are also found in modern theropods (that is, the birds), are interpreted as having held <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parabronchi" title="Parabronchi">air sacs</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> The rib cage was broad, giving it a barrel chest, especially in comparison to less <a href="/wiki/Synapomorphy" title="Synapomorphy">derived</a> theropods like <i>Ceratosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88b_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88b-18">[18]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> had <a href="/wiki/Gastralium" title="Gastralium">gastralia</a> (belly ribs), but these are not common findings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> and they may have <a href="/wiki/Ossification" title="Ossification">ossified</a> poorly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> In one published case, the gastralia show evidence of injury during life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00b_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00b-19">[19]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Furcula" title="Furcula">furcula</a> (wishbone) was also present, but has only been recognized since 1996; in some cases furculae were confused with gastralia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00b_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00b-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM96_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CM96-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a>, the main hip bone, was massive, and the <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubic bone</a> had a prominent foot that may have been used for both muscle attachment and as a prop for resting the body on the ground. Madsen noted that in about half of the individuals from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Dinosaur_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry</a>, independent of size, the pubes had not fused to each other at their foot ends. He suggested that this was a <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual characteristic</a>, with females lacking fused bones to make egg-laying easier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> This proposal has not attracted further attention, however.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG/220px-Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG/330px-Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG/440px-Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus-fragilis-Klauen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hand and claws of <i>A. fragilis</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The forelimbs of <i>Allosaurus</i> were short in comparison to the hindlimbs (only about 35% the length of the hindlimbs in adults)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MG98_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MG98-21">[21]</a></sup> and had three fingers per hand, tipped with large, strongly curved and pointed <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">claws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> The arms were powerful,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> and the forearm was somewhat shorter than the upper arm (1:1.2 <a href="/wiki/Ulna" title="Ulna">ulna</a>/<a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> ratio).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> The wrist had a version of the semilunate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpus" title="Carpus">carpal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC02_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC02-23">[23]</a></sup> also found in more derived theropods like <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">maniraptorans</a>. Of the three fingers, the innermost (or <a href="/wiki/Thumb" title="Thumb">thumb</a>) was the largest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> and diverged from the others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> The phalangeal formula is 2-3-4-0-0, meaning that the innermost finger (phalange) has two bones, the next has three, and the third finger has four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The legs were not as long or suited for speed as those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurid" title="Tyrannosaurid">tyrannosaurids</a>, and the claws of the toes were less developed and more <a href="/wiki/Hoof" title="Hoof">hoof</a>-like than those of earlier theropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> Each foot had three weight-bearing toes and an inner <a href="/wiki/Dewclaw" title="Dewclaw">dewclaw</a>, which Madsen suggested could have been used for grasping in juveniles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> There was also what is interpreted as the splint-like remnant of a fifth (outermost) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metatarsus" title="Metatarsus">metatarsal</a>, perhaps used as a lever between the <a href="/wiki/Achilles_tendon" title="Achilles tendon">Achilles tendon</a> and foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88d_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88d-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> was an allosaurid, a member of a <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> of large theropods within the larger group <a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">Carnosauria</a>. The family name <a href="/wiki/Allosauridae" title="Allosauridae">Allosauridae</a> was created for this genus in 1878 by <a href="/wiki/Othniel_Charles_Marsh" title="Othniel Charles Marsh">Othniel Charles Marsh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM78_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCM78-26">[26]</a></sup> but the term was largely unused until the 1970s in favor of <a href="/wiki/Megalosauridae" title="Megalosauridae">Megalosauridae</a>, another family of large theropods that eventually became a <a href="/wiki/Wastebasket_taxon" title="Wastebasket taxon">wastebasket taxon</a>. This, along with the use of <i>Antrodemus</i> for <i>Allosaurus</i> during the same period, is a point that needs to be remembered when searching for information on <i>Allosaurus</i> in publications that predate James Madsen's 1976 monograph. Major publications using the name "Megalosauridae" instead of "Allosauridae" include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Gilmore" title="Charles Gilmore">Gilmore</a>, 1920,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Huene" title="Friedrich von Huene">von Huene</a>, 1926,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FvH26_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FvH26-27">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_Sherwood_Romer" title="Alfred Sherwood Romer">Romer</a>, 1956 and 1966,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASR56_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASR56-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASR66_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASR66-29">[29]</a></sup> Steel, 1970,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steel70_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steel70-30">[30]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alick_Walker" title="Alick Walker">Walker</a>, 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW64_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADW64-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2277" data-file-width="2818" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg/220px-WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg/330px-WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg/440px-WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WLA_hmns_Allosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mounted skeleton of "Big Al II" (specimen SMA 0005)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saurophaganax.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saurophaganax.jpg/220px-Saurophaganax.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saurophaganax.jpg/330px-Saurophaganax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Saurophaganax.jpg/440px-Saurophaganax.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saurophaganax.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restored skeleton of <i>Saurophaganax</i> or <i>A. maximus</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the publication of Madsen's influential monograph, Allosauridae became the preferred family assignment, but it too was not strongly defined. Semi-technical works used Allosauridae for a variety of large theropods, usually those that were larger and better-known than megalosaurids. Typical theropods that were thought to be related to <i>Allosaurus</i> included <i><a href="/wiki/Indosaurus" title="Indosaurus">Indosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Piatnitzkysaurus" title="Piatnitzkysaurus">Piatnitzkysaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Piveteausaurus" title="Piveteausaurus">Piveteausaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Yangchuanosaurus" title="Yangchuanosaurus">Yangchuanosaurus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL83_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-DL83-32">[32]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Acrocanthosaurus" title="Acrocanthosaurus">Acrocanthosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Chilantaisaurus" title="Chilantaisaurus">Chilantaisaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Compsosuchus" title="Compsosuchus">Compsosuchus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Stokesosaurus" title="Stokesosaurus">Stokesosaurus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Szechuanosaurus" title="Szechuanosaurus">Szechuanosaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL90_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-DL90-33">[33]</a></sup> Given modern knowledge of theropod diversity and the advent of cladistic study of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> relationships, none of these theropods is now recognized as an allosaurid, although several, like <i>Acrocanthosaurus</i> and <i>Yangchuanosaurus</i>, are members of closely related families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Below is a cladogram by Benson <i>et al.</i> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bensonetal2010_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-bensonetal2010-34">[34]</a></sup></p>

<p><br/>
Allosauridae is one of four families in Carnosauria; the other three are <a href="/wiki/Neovenatoridae" title="Neovenatoridae">Neovenatoridae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bensonetal2010_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-bensonetal2010-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauridae" title="Carcharodontosauridae">Carcharodontosauridae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinraptoridae" title="Sinraptoridae">Sinraptoridae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> Allosauridae has at times been proposed as ancestral to the <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosauridae" title="Tyrannosauridae">Tyrannosauridae</a> (which would make it <a href="/wiki/Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a>), one recent example being Gregory S. Paul's <i>Predatory Dinosaurs of the World</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88c_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88c-35">[35]</a></sup> but this has been rejected, with tyrannosaurids identified as members of a separate branch of theropods, the <a href="/wiki/Coelurosauria" title="Coelurosauria">Coelurosauria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TRHJ94_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-TRHJ94-36">[36]</a></sup> Allosauridae is the smallest of the carnosaur families, with only <i>Saurophaganax</i> and a currently unnamed <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Allosauroidea" title="Allosauroidea">allosauroid</a> accepted as possible valid <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> besides <i>Allosaurus</i> in the most recent review.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> Another genus, <i>Epanterias</i>, is a potential valid member, but it and <i>Saurophaganax</i> may turn out to be large examples of <i>Allosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-8"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> Recent reviews have kept the genus <i>Saurophaganax</i> and included <i>Epanterias</i> with <i>Allosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRF03_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-JRF03-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_history">Discovery and history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Discovery and history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1446" data-file-width="1002" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg/170px-Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg/255px-Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg/340px-Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_by_Marsh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restoration of the pelvis, fore and hindlimbs based on specimens O. C. Marsh had assigned to <i>A. fragilis</i> by 1884</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_discoveries_and_research">Early discoveries and research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early discoveries and research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The discovery and early study of <i>Allosaurus</i> is complicated by the multiplicity of names coined during the <a href="/wiki/Bone_Wars" title="Bone Wars">Bone Wars</a> of the late 19th century. The first described <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> in this history was a bone obtained secondhand by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Vandiveer_Hayden" title="Ferdinand Vandiveer Hayden">Ferdinand Vandiveer Hayden</a> in 1869. It came from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Park_(Colorado_basin)" title="Middle Park (Colorado basin)">Middle Park</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Granby,_Colorado" title="Granby, Colorado">Granby, Colorado</a>, probably from <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a> rocks. The locals had identified such bones as "petrified <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a> hoofs". Hayden sent his specimen to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Leidy" title="Joseph Leidy">Joseph Leidy</a>, who identified it as half of a tail vertebra, and tentatively assigned it to the European dinosaur genus <i><a href="/wiki/Poekilopleuron" title="Poekilopleuron">Poekilopleuron</a></i> as <i>Poicilopleuron</i>  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] <i>valens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JL70_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-JL70-37">[37]</a></sup> He later decided it deserved its own genus, <i>Antrodemus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JL73_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-JL73-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2388" data-file-width="4501" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg/200px-AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg/300px-AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg/400px-AMNH_Allosaurus.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Mounted <i>A. fragilis</i> specimen (AMNH 5753), posed as scavenging an <i><a href="/wiki/Apatosaurus" title="Apatosaurus">Apatosaurus</a></i></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus4.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="700" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Allosaurus4.jpg/200px-Allosaurus4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Allosaurus4.jpg/300px-Allosaurus4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Allosaurus4.jpg/400px-Allosaurus4.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">AMNH 5753 in a <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Knight" title="Charles R. Knight">Charles R. Knight</a> life restoration</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> itself is <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">based on</a> <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">YPM</a> 1930, a small collection of fragmentary bones including parts of three vertebrae, a rib fragment, a tooth, a toe bone, and, most useful for later discussions, the shaft of the right humerus (upper arm). Othniel Charles Marsh gave these remains the formal name <i>Allosaurus fragilis</i> in 1877. <i>Allosaurus</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i>allos/αλλος</i>, meaning "strange" or "different" and <i>sauros/σαυρος</i>, meaning "lizard" or "reptile".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It was named 'different lizard' because its vertebrae were different from those of other dinosaurs known at the time of its discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM77_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCM77-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMLomnipedia_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMLomnipedia-41">[41]</a></sup> The species epithet <i>fragilis</i> is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "fragile", referring to lightening features in the vertebrae. The bones were collected from the Morrison Formation of <a href="/wiki/Garden_Park,_Colorado" title="Garden Park, Colorado">Garden Park</a>, north of <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1on_City,_Colorado" title="Cañon City, Colorado">Cañon City</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM77_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCM77-40">[40]</a></sup> Marsh and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Drinker_Cope" title="Edward Drinker Cope">Edward Drinker Cope</a>, who were in scientific competition, went on to coin several other genera based on similarly sparse material that would later figure in the taxonomy of <i>Allosaurus</i>. These include Marsh's <i>Creosaurus</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM78_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCM78-26">[26]</a></sup> and <i>Labrosaurus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM79_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCM79-42">[42]</a></sup> and Cope's <i>Epanterias</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDC78_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-EDC78-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In their haste, Cope and Marsh did not always follow up on their discoveries (or, more commonly, those made by their subordinates). For example, after the discovery by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Mudge" title="Benjamin Franklin Mudge">Benjamin Mudge</a> of the type specimen of <i>Allosaurus</i> in Colorado, Marsh elected to concentrate work in <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>; when work resumed at Garden Park in 1883, M. P. Felch found an almost complete <i>Allosaurus</i> and several partial skeletons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBN85_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-DBN85-14">[14]</a></sup> In addition, one of Cope's collectors, H. F. Hubbell, found a specimen in the <a href="/wiki/Como_Bluff" title="Como Bluff">Como Bluff</a> area of Wyoming in 1879, but apparently did not mention its completeness, and Cope never unpacked it. Upon unpacking in 1903 (several years after Cope had died), it was found to be one of the most complete theropod specimens then known, and in 1908 the skeleton, now cataloged as AMNH 5753, was put on public view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGD95_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGD95-44">[44]</a></sup> This is the well-known mount poised over a partial <i><a href="/wiki/Apatosaurus" title="Apatosaurus">Apatosaurus</a></i> skeleton as if <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenging</a> it, illustrated as such by Charles R. Knight. Although notable as the first free-standing mount of a theropod dinosaur, and often illustrated and photographed, it has never been scientifically described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBetal99_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBetal99-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>The multiplicity of early names complicated later research, with the situation compounded by the terse descriptions provided by Marsh and Cope. Even at the time, authors such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Wendell_Williston" title="Samuel Wendell Williston">Samuel Wendell Williston</a> suggested that too many names had been coined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWW78_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWW78-46">[46]</a></sup> For example, Williston pointed out in 1901 that Marsh had never been able to adequately distinguish <i>Allosaurus</i> from <i>Creosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWW01_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWW01-47">[47]</a></sup> The most influential early attempt to sort out the convoluted situation was produced by <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Gilmore" title="Charles W. Gilmore">Charles W. Gilmore</a> in 1920. He came to the conclusion that the tail vertebra named <i>Antrodemus</i> by Leidy was indistinguishable from those of <i>Allosaurus</i>, and <i>Antrodemus</i> thus should be the preferred name because as the older name it had priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> <i>Antrodemus</i> became the accepted name for this familiar genus for over fifty years, until James Madsen published on the Cleveland-Lloyd specimens and concluded that <i>Allosaurus</i> should be used because <i>Antrodemus</i> was based on material with poor, if any, diagnostic features and locality information (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Geological_formation" title="Geological formation">geological formation</a> that the single bone of <i>Antrodemus</i> came from is unknown).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-14"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> "<i>Antrodemus</i>" has been used informally for convenience when distinguishing between the skull Gilmore restored and the composite skull restored by Madsen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DH98_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-DH98-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleveland-Lloyd_discoveries">Cleveland-Lloyd discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cleveland-Lloyd discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1351" data-file-width="2401" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_atrox_Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Specimen at the Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry museum, Utah</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although sporadic work at what became known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Dinosaur_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Emery_County,_Utah" title="Emery County, Utah">Emery County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> had taken place as early as 1927, and the fossil site itself described by William J. Stokes in 1945,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJS45_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-WJS45-49">[49]</a></sup> major operations did not begin there until 1960. Under a cooperative effort involving nearly 40 institutions, thousands of bones were recovered between 1960 and 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-15"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> The quarry is notable for the predominance of <i>Allosaurus</i> remains, the condition of the specimens, and the lack of scientific resolution on how it came to be. The majority of bones belong to the large theropod <i>Allosaurus fragilis</i> (it is estimated that the remains of at least 46 <i>A. fragilis</i> have been found there, out of at minimum 73 dinosaurs), and the fossils found there are disarticulated and well-mixed. Nearly a dozen scientific papers have been written on the <a href="/wiki/Taphonomy" title="Taphonomy">taphonomy</a> of the site, suggesting numerous mutually exclusive explanations for how it may have formed. Suggestions have ranged from animals getting stuck in a bog, to becoming trapped in deep mud, to falling victim to <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>-induced mortality around a waterhole, to getting trapped in a spring-fed pond or seep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APHetal06_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-APHetal06-50">[50]</a></sup> Regardless of the actual cause, the great quantity of well-preserved <i>Allosaurus</i> remains has allowed this genus to be known in detail, making it among the best-known theropods. Skeletal remains from the quarry pertain to individuals of almost all ages and sizes, from less than 1 meter (3.3 feet)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSCC03_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSCC03-51">[51]</a></sup> to 12 meters (39 feet) long, and the disarticulation is an advantage for describing bones usually found fused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-16"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_work:_1980s.E2.80.93present">Recent work: 1980s–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recent work: 1980s–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The period since Madsen's monograph has been marked by a great expansion in studies dealing with topics concerning <i>Allosaurus</i> in life (<a href="/wiki/Paleobiology" title="Paleobiology">paleobiological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleoecology" title="Paleoecology">paleoecological</a> topics). Such studies have covered topics including skeletal variation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DKS98_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-DKS98-52">[52]</a></sup> growth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBetal06_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-PBetal06-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FC06_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FC06-54">[54]</a></sup> skull construction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERetal01_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ERetal01-55">[55]</a></sup> hunting methods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB98_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-BB98-56">[56]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWR99_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SWR99-57">[57]</a></sup> and the possibility of gregarious living and parental care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTB97_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTB97-58">[58]</a></sup> Reanalysis of old material (particularly of large 'allosaur' specimens),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-9"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC95_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJC95-59">[59]</a></sup> new discoveries in Portugal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMetal99_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMetal99-60">[60]</a></sup> and several very complete new specimens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00b_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00b-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB96_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-BB96-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BigAlTwo_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-BigAlTwo-62">[62]</a></sup> have also contributed to the growing knowledge base.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Big_Al.22_and_.22Big_Al_Two.22">"Big Al" and "Big Al Two"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title='Edit section: "Big Al" and "Big Al Two"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1359" data-file-width="2931" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg/220px-Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg/330px-Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg/440px-Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Big_Al_Allosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Big Al" at <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Rockies" title="Museum of the Rockies">Museum of the Rockies</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1991 "Big Al" (<a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Rockies" title="Museum of the Rockies">MOR</a> 693), a 95% complete, partially articulated specimen of <i>Allosaurus</i> was discovered. It measured about 8 meters (about 26 ft) in length. MOR 693 was excavated near <a href="/wiki/Shell,_Wyoming" title="Shell, Wyoming">Shell, Wyoming</a>, by a joint Museum of the Rockies and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming">University of Wyoming</a> Geological Museum team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBbigal_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBbigal-63">[63]</a></sup> This skeleton was discovered by a Swiss team, led by Kirby Siber. In 1996 the same team discovered a second <i>Allosaurus</i>, "Big Al Two", which is the best preserved skeleton of its kind to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BigAlTwo_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-BigAlTwo-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The completeness, preservation, and scientific importance of this skeleton gave "Big Al" its name; the individual itself was below the average size for <i>Allosaurus fragilis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBbigal_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBbigal-63">[63]</a></sup> and was a subadult estimated at only 87% grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RRH02_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-RRH02-64">[64]</a></sup> The specimen was described by Breithaupt in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB96_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-BB96-61">[61]</a></sup> Nineteen of its bones were broken or showed signs of <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a>, which may have contributed to "Big Al's" death. <a href="/wiki/Paleopathology" title="Paleopathology">Pathologic</a> bones included five ribs, five vertebrae, and four bones of the feet; several damaged bones showed <a href="/wiki/Osteomyelitis" title="Osteomyelitis">osteomyelitis</a>, a bone infection. A particular problem for the living animal was infection and trauma to the right foot that probably affected movement and may have also predisposed the other foot to injury because of a change in gait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RRH02_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-RRH02-64">[64]</a></sup> Al had an infection on the first phalanx on the third toe that was afflicted by an <a href="/wiki/Involucrum" title="Involucrum">involucrum</a>. The infection was long lived, perhaps up to 6 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Big Al Two is also known to have multiple injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_and_taxonomy">Species and taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Species and taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Species_of_Allosaurus" title="Species of Allosaurus">Species of Allosaurus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="313" data-file-width="472" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg/220px-Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg/330px-Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg/440px-Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus-fossilized_skull.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skull of "A. jimmadseni" (DINO 11541) from <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_National_Monument" title="Dinosaur National Monument">Dinosaur National Monument</a>, when it was still partially encased in matrix</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are currently four valid and one undescribed species of <i>Allosaurus</i> (<i>A. amplus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <i>A. europaeus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMetal06_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-OMetal06-68">[68]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Type_species" title="Type species">type species</a> <i>A. fragilis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> the as-yet not formally described "A. jimmadseni",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00-10">[10]</a></sup> and <i>A. lucasi</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>).</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antrodemus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="622" data-file-width="798" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antrodemus.jpg/220px-Antrodemus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antrodemus.jpg/330px-Antrodemus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Antrodemus.jpg/440px-Antrodemus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antrodemus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Antrodemus valens</i> holotype tail vertebra (above) compared to same of <i>Allosaurus</i> (below)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>A. fragilis</i>, "A. jimmadseni", <i>A. amplus</i>, and <i>A. lucasi</i> are all known from remains discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Kimmeridgian" title="Kimmeridgian">Kimmeridgian</a>–<a href="/wiki/Tithonian" title="Tithonian">Tithonian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Jurassic" title="Upper Jurassic">Upper Jurassic</a>-age Morrison Formation of the United States, spread across the states of <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, Utah, and Wyoming. <i>A. fragilis</i> is regarded as the most common, known from the remains of at least sixty individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> For a while in the late 1980s and early 1990s it was common to recognize <i>A. fragilis</i> as the short-snouted species, with the long-snouted taxon being <i>A. atrox</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-10"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG93_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-LG93-70">[70]</a></sup> however, subsequent analysis of specimens from the Cleveland-Lloyd Quarry, Como Bluff, and Dry Mesa Quarry showed that the differences seen in the Morrison Formation material could be attributed to individual variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DKS96_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-DKS96-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DKS99_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-DKS99-72">[72]</a></sup> A study of skull elements from the Cleveland-Lloyd site found wide variation between individuals, calling into question previous species-level distinctions based on such features as the shape of the lacrimal horns, and the proposed differentiation of "A. jimmadseni" based on the shape of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jugal" title="Jugal">jugal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC2010_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC2010-73">[73]</a></sup> <i>A. europaeus</i> was found in the Kimmeridgian-age Porto Novo Member of the <a href="/wiki/Lourinh%C3%A3_Formation" title="Lourinhã Formation">Lourinhã Formation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMetal06_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-OMetal06-68">[68]</a></sup> but may be the same as <i>A. fragilis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MDOE07_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-MDOE07-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Allosaurus tendagurensis</i> was found in Kimmeridgian-age rocks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tendaguru" title="Tendaguru">Tendaguru</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Mtwara_Region" title="Mtwara Region">Mtwara</a>, Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJ25_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-WJ25-75">[75]</a></sup> Subsequent studies classified it as a non-coelurosaurian tetanuran, either a megalosaurid or carcharodontosaur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OWMR05_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-OWMR05-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OR2011_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-OR2011-77">[77]</a></sup> Although obscure, it was a large theropod, possibly around 10 meters (33 feet) long and 2.5 metric tons (2.8 short tons) in weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMDML03_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-MMDML03-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Creosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="936" data-file-width="1292" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Creosaurus.jpg/220px-Creosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Creosaurus.jpg/330px-Creosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Creosaurus.jpg/440px-Creosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Creosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Holotype material of <i>Creosaurus atrox</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> is regarded as senior synonym of the genera <i>Creosaurus</i>, <i>Epanterias</i>, and <i>Labrosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> Most of the species that are regarded as synonyms of <i>A. fragilis</i>, or that were misassigned to the genus, are obscure and were based on scrappy remains. One exception is <i>Labrosaurus ferox</i>, named in 1884 by Marsh for an oddly formed partial lower jaw, with a prominent gap in the tooth row at the tip of the jaw, and a rear section greatly expanded and turned down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCM84_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCM84-78">[78]</a></sup> Later researchers suggested that the bone was <a href="/wiki/Pathology" title="Pathology">pathologic</a>, showing an injury to the living animal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> and that part of the unusual form of the rear of the bone was due to plaster reconstruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW00_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-MW00-79">[79]</a></sup> It is now regarded as an example of <i>A. fragilis.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-8"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other remains formerly thought to pertain to <i>Allosaurus</i> were described from <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MFR81_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-MFR81-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANNR14_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANNR14-81">[81]</a></sup> but these fossils have been reassessed as belonging to other dinosaurs.</p>
<p>The issue of synonyms is complicated by the <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a> of <i>Allosaurus fragilis</i> (catalog number <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Peabody Museum of Natural History">YPM</a> 1930) being extremely fragmentary, consisting of a few incomplete vertebrae, limb bone fragments, rib fragments, and a tooth. Because of this, several scientists have interpreted the type specimen as potentially dubious, and thus the genus <i>Allosaurus</i> itself or at least the species <i>A. fragilis</i> would be a <i>nomen dubium</i> ("dubious name", based on a specimen too incomplete to compare to other specimens or to classify). To address this situation, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_S._Paul" title="Gregory S. Paul">Gregory S. Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Carpenter" title="Kenneth Carpenter">Kenneth Carpenter</a> (2010) submitted a petition to the <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_on_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Commission on Zoological Nomenclature">ICZN</a> to have the name "<i>A. fragilis"</i> officially transferred to the more complete specimen USNM4734 (as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hapantotype" title="Hapantotype">neotype</a>). This request is currently pending review.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GPKC2010_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-GPKC2010-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleobiology">Paleobiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Paleobiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_history">Life history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Life history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG/220px-Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG/330px-Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG/440px-Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_displays_-_Natural_History_Museum_of_Utah_-_DSC07215.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skeletons at different growth stages</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The wealth of <i>Allosaurus</i> fossils, from nearly all ages of individuals, allows scientists to study how the animal grew and how long its lifespan may have been. Remains may reach as far back in the lifespan as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a>—crushed eggs from Colorado have been suggested as those of <i>Allosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG97_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-DFG97-4">[4]</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">histological</a> analysis of limb bones, bone deposition appears to stop at around 22 to 28 years, which is comparable to that of other large theropods like <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>. From the same analysis, its maximum growth appears to have been at age 15, with an estimated growth rate of about 150 kilograms (330 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a>) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBetal06_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-PBetal06-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Medullary bone tissue (endosteally derived, ephemeral, mineralization located inside the medulla of the long bones in gravid female birds) has been reported in at least one <i>Allosaurus</i> specimen, a <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">shin bone</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Quarry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LW08_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-LW08-83">[83]</a></sup> Today, this bone tissue is only formed in female birds that are laying eggs, as it is used to supply <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> to shells. Its presence in the <i>Allosaurus</i> individual has been used to establish sex and show it had reached reproductive age. However, other studies have called into question some cases of medullary bone in dinosaurs, including this <i>Allosaurus</i> individual. Data from extant birds suggested that the medullary bone in this <i>Allosaurus</i> individual may have been the result of a bone pathology instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chin09_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chin09-84">[84]</a></sup> However, with the confirmation of medullary tissue indicating gender in a specimen of Tyrannosaurus, it may be possible to ascertain whether or not the Allosaurus in question was indeed female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2382" data-file-width="3434" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Juvenile_Reconstruction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restoration of a juvenile</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The discovery of a juvenile specimen with a nearly complete hindlimb shows that the legs were relatively longer in juveniles, and the lower segments of the leg (shin and foot) were relatively longer than the thigh. These differences suggest that younger <i>Allosaurus</i> were faster and had different hunting strategies than adults, perhaps chasing small prey as juveniles, then becoming ambush hunters of large prey upon adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FC06_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FC06-54">[54]</a></sup> The thigh bone became thicker and wider during growth, and the cross-section less circular, as muscle attachments shifted, muscles became shorter, and the growth of the leg slowed. These changes imply that juvenile legs has less predictable stresses compared with adults, which would have moved with more regular forward progression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCS02_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-LCS02-86">[86]</a></sup> Conversely, the skull bones appear to have generally grown <a href="/wiki/Allometry" title="Allometry">isometrically</a>, increasing in size without changing in proportion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC2010_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-KC2010-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3960" data-file-width="3240" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg/170px-Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg/255px-Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg/340px-Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_attacks_Stegosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Allosaurus</i> and <i>Stegosaurus</i> skeletons, <a href="/wiki/Denver_Museum_of_Nature_and_Science" title="Denver Museum of Nature and Science">Denver Museum of Nature and Science</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Paleontologists accept <i>Allosaurus</i> as an active predator of large animals. There is dramatic evidence for allosaur attacks on <i>Stegosaurus</i>, including an <i>Allosaurus</i> tail vertebra with a partially healed puncture wound that fits a <i>Stegosaurus</i> <a href="/wiki/Thagomizer" title="Thagomizer">tail spike</a>, and a <i>Stegosaurus</i> neck plate with a U-shaped wound that correlates well with an <i>Allosaurus</i> snout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KSMW05_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-KSMW05-87">[87]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropod" title="Sauropod">Sauropods</a> seem to be likely candidates as both live prey and as objects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scavenging" title="Scavenging">scavenging</a>, based on the presence of scrapings on sauropod bones fitting allosaur teeth well and the presence of shed allosaur teeth with sauropod bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FS04_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FS04-88">[88]</a></sup> However, as Gregory Paul noted in 1988, <i>Allosaurus</i> was probably not a predator of fully grown sauropods, unless it hunted in packs, as it had a modestly sized skull and relatively small teeth, and was greatly outweighed by contemporaneous sauropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_12-11"><a href="#cite_note-GSP88-12">[12]</a></sup> Another possibility is that it preferred to hunt juveniles instead of fully grown adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF07_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-JF07-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG93_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-LG93-70">[70]</a></sup> Research in the 1990s and first decade of the 21st century may have found other solutions to this question. <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Robert T. Bakker</a>, comparing <i>Allosaurus</i> to <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a> sabre-toothed carnivorous mammals, found similar adaptations, such as a reduction of jaw muscles and increase in neck muscles, and the ability to open the jaws extremely wide. Although <i>Allosaurus</i> did not have sabre teeth, Bakker suggested another mode of attack that would have used such neck and jaw adaptations: the short teeth in effect became small serrations on a <a href="/wiki/Saw" title="Saw">saw</a>-like cutting edge running the length of the upper jaw, which would have been driven into prey. This type of jaw would permit slashing attacks against much larger prey, with the goal of weakening the victim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB98_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-BB98-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_Jaws_Steveoc86.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Allosaurus</i> attacking, based on the theories of <a href="/wiki/Robert_T._Bakker" title="Robert T. Bakker">Bakker</a> (1998) and <a href="/wiki/Emily_Rayfield" title="Emily Rayfield">Rayfield</a> <i>et al.</i> (2001).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Similar conclusions were drawn by another study using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finite_element_analysis" title="Finite element analysis">finite element analysis</a> on an <i>Allosaurus</i> skull. According to their biomechanical analysis, the skull was very strong but had a relatively small bite force. By using jaw muscles only, it could produce a bite force of 805 to 2,148 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a>, less than the values for <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligators</a> (13,000 N), <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lions</a> (4,167 N), and <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopards</a> (2,268 N), but the skull could withstand nearly 55,500 N of vertical force against the tooth row. The authors suggested that <i>Allosaurus</i> used its skull like a hatchet against prey, attacking open-mouthed, slashing flesh with its teeth, and tearing it away without splintering bones, unlike <i>Tyrannosaurus</i>, which is thought to have been capable of damaging bones. They also suggested that the architecture of the skull could have permitted the use of different strategies against different prey; the skull was light enough to allow attacks on smaller and more agile ornithopods, but strong enough for high-impact ambush attacks against larger prey like stegosaurids and sauropods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERetal01_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-ERetal01-55">[55]</a></sup> Their interpretations were challenged by other researchers, who found no modern analogues to a hatchet attack and considered it more likely that the skull was strong to compensate for its open construction when absorbing the stresses from struggling prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FK02_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FK02-89">[89]</a></sup> The original authors noted that <i>Allosaurus</i> itself has no modern equivalent, that the tooth row is well-suited to such an attack, and that articulations in the skull cited by their detractors as problematic actually helped protect the <a href="/wiki/Palate" title="Palate">palate</a> and lessen stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ERetal02_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ERetal02-90">[90]</a></sup> Another possibility for handling large prey is that theropods like <i>Allosaurus</i> were "flesh grazers" which could take bites of flesh out of living sauropods that were sufficient to sustain the predator so it would not have needed to expend the effort to kill the prey outright. This strategy would also potentially have allowed the prey to recover and be fed upon in a similar way later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-9"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> An additional suggestion notes that ornithopods were the most common available dinosaurian prey, and that allosaurs may have subdued them by using an attack similar to that of modern big cats: grasping the prey with their forelimbs, and then making multiple bites on the throat to crush the trachea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF07_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-JF07-8">[8]</a></sup> This is compatible with other evidence that the forelimbs were strong and capable of restraining prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC02_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-KC02-23">[23]</a></sup> Studies done by Stephen Lautenschager <i>et al.</i> from the University of Bristol also indicate <i>Allosaurus</i> could open its jaws quite wide and sustain considerable muscle force. When compared with <i>Tyrannosaurus</i> and the therizinosaurid <i><a href="/wiki/Erlikosaurus" title="Erlikosaurus">Erlikosaurus</a></i> in the same study, it was found that <i>Allosaurus</i> had a wider gape than either; the animal was capable of opening its jaws to a 79 degree angle. The findings also indicate that large carnivorous dinosaurs, like modern carnivores, had wider jaw gapes than herbivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanical</a> study published in 2013 by Eric Snively and colleagues found that <i>Allosaurus</i> had an unusually low attachment point on the skull for the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Longissimus_capitis_superficialis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Longissimus capitis superficialis (page does not exist)">longissimus capitis superficialis</a> neck muscle compared to other theropods such as <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>. This would have allowed the animal to make rapid and forceful vertical movements with the skull. The authors found that vertical strikes as proposed by Bakker and Rayfield are consistent with the animal's capabilities. They also found that the animal probably processed carcasses by vertical movements in a similar manner to <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcons</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Kestrel" title="Kestrel">kestrels</a>: the animal could have gripped prey with the skull and feet, then pulled back and up to remove flesh. This differs from the prey-handling envisioned for tyrannosaurids, which probably tore flesh with lateral shakes of the skull, similar to crocodilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESetal2013_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESetal2013-93">[93]</a></sup> In addition, <i>Allosaurus</i> was able to "move its head and neck around relatively rapidly and with considerable control", at the cost of power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scidaily2013_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scidaily2013-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
