<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1354" data-file-width="810" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg/170px-Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg/255px-Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg/340px-Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_skull_front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>A. fragilis</i> skull cast in front view at the Museum für Naturkunde, Berlin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other aspects of feeding include the eyes, arms, and legs. The shape of the skull of <i>Allosaurus</i> limited potential <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a> to 20° of width, slightly less than that of modern <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a>. As with crocodilians, this may have been enough to judge prey distance and time attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KAS06_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-KAS06-95">[95]</a></sup> The arms, compared with those of other theropods, were suited for both grasping prey at a distance or clutching it close,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC02_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-KC02-23">[23]</a></sup> and the articulation of the claws suggests that they could have been used to hook things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CWG20_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-CWG20-22">[22]</a></sup> Finally, the top speed of <i>Allosaurus</i> has been estimated at 30 to 55 kilometers per hour (19 to 34 miles per hour).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PC98_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-PC98-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A new study on the skull of <i>Allosaurus</i> and how it worked has deemed the hatchet jaw attack unlikely since the animal would be wasting great amounts of energy with each bite. It seems more likely that <i>Allosaurus</i> hunted prey like any typical carnosaur by ripping great chunks of flesh from the animal and letting it bleed to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_behavior">Social behavior</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Social behavior">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Labrosaurus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="605" data-file-width="748" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Labrosaurus.jpg/220px-Labrosaurus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Labrosaurus.jpg/330px-Labrosaurus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Labrosaurus.jpg/440px-Labrosaurus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Labrosaurus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The holotype dentary of <i>Labrosaurus ferox</i>, which may have been injured by the bite of another <i>A. fragilis</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It has been speculated since the 1970s that <i>Allosaurus</i> preyed on sauropods and other large dinosaurs by hunting in groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF76_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-JF76-98">[98]</a></sup> Such a depiction is common in semitechnical and popular dinosaur literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBN85_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-DBN85-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL83_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-DL83-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LG93_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-LG93-70">[70]</a></sup> Robert T. Bakker has extended social behavior to parental care, and has interpreted shed allosaur teeth and chewed bones of large prey animals as evidence that adult allosaurs brought food to lairs for their young to eat until they were grown, and prevented other carnivores from scavenging on the food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTB97_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-RTB97-58">[58]</a></sup> However, there is actually little evidence of gregarious behavior in theropods,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_17-10"><a href="#cite_note-HMC04-17">[17]</a></sup> and social interactions with members of the same species would have included antagonistic encounters, as shown by injuries to gastralia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJC00b_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-DJC00b-19">[19]</a></sup> and bite wounds to skulls (the pathologic lower jaw named <i>Labrosaurus ferox</i> is one such possible example). Such head-biting may have been a way to establish dominance in a <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">pack</a> or to settle <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a> disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TC98_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-TC98-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although <i>Allosaurus</i> may have hunted in packs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-completedino_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-completedino-100">[100]</a></sup> it has been argued that <i>Allosaurus</i> and other theropods had largely aggressive interactions instead of cooperative interactions with other members of their own species. The study in question noted that cooperative hunting of prey much larger than an individual predator, as is commonly inferred for theropod dinosaurs, is rare among <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> in general, and modern <a href="/wiki/Diapsid" title="Diapsid">diapsid</a> carnivores (including lizards, crocodiles, and birds) very rarely cooperate to hunt in such a way. Instead, they are typically territorial and will kill and <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_(zoology)" title="Cannibalism (zoology)">cannibalize</a> intruders of the same species, and will also do the same to smaller individuals that attempt to eat before they do when aggregated at feeding sites. According to this interpretation, the accumulation of remains of multiple <i>Allosaurus</i> individuals at the same site, e.g. in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland%E2%80%93Lloyd_Quarry" title="Cleveland–Lloyd Quarry">Cleveland–Lloyd Quarry</a>, are not due to pack hunting, but to the fact that <i>Allosaurus</i> individuals were drawn together to feed on other disabled or dead allosaurs, and were sometimes killed in the process. This could explain the high proportion of juvenile and subadult allosaurs present, as juveniles and subadults are disproportionally killed at modern group feeding sites of animals like <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Komodo_dragon" title="Komodo dragon">Komodo dragons</a>. The same interpretation applies to Bakker's lair sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RB07_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-RB07-101">[101]</a></sup> There is some evidence for cannibalism in <i>Allosaurus</i>, including <i>Allosaurus</i> shed teeth found among rib fragments, possible tooth marks on a <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">shoulder blade</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGD04_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-BGD04-102">[102]</a></sup> and cannibalized allosaur skeletons among the bones at Bakker's lair sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB04_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-BB04-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_and_senses">Brain and senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Brain and senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The brain of <i>Allosaurus</i>, as interpreted from spiral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computed_tomography" title="Computed tomography">CT scanning</a> of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocranial_cast" title="Endocranial cast">endocast</a>, was more consistent with <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilian</a> brains than those of the other living <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">archosaurs</a>, birds. The structure of the <a href="/wiki/Vestibular_system" title="Vestibular system">vestibular apparatus</a> indicates that the skull was held nearly horizontal, as opposed to strongly tipped up or down. The structure of the <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">inner ear</a> was like that of a crocodilian, and so <i>Allosaurus</i> probably could have heard lower frequencies best, and would have had trouble with subtle sounds. The <a href="/wiki/Olfactory_bulb" title="Olfactory bulb">olfactory bulbs</a> were large and seem to have been well suited for detecting odors, although the area for evaluating smells was relatively small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SWR99_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-SWR99-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleopathology">Paleopathology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Paleopathology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1493" data-file-width="2227" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_fragilis_USNM4734.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mounted <i>A. fragilis</i> skeleton (USNM4734), which has several healed injuries</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2001, Bruce Rothschild and others published a study examining evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_fractures" title="Stress fractures">stress fractures</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tendon_avulsions" title="Tendon avulsions">tendon avulsions</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theropod" title="Theropod">theropod</a> dinosaurs and the implications for their behavior. Since stress fractures are caused by repeated trauma rather than singular events they are more likely to be caused by the behavior of the animal than other kinds of injury. Stress fractures and tendon avulsions occurring in the forelimb have special behavioral significance since while injuries to the feet could be caused by running or <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migration</a>, resistant prey items are the most probable source of injuries to the hand. <i>Allosaurus</i> was one of only two theropods examined in the study to exhibit a tendon avulsion, and in both cases the avulsion occurred on the forelimb. When the researchers looked for stress fractures, they found that <i>Allosaurus</i> had a significantly greater number of stress fractures than <i><a href="/wiki/Albertosaurus" title="Albertosaurus">Albertosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ornithomimus" title="Ornithomimus">Ornithomimus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeornithomimus" title="Archaeornithomimus">Archaeornithomimus</a></i>. Of the 47 hand bones the researchers studied, 3 were found to contain stress fractures. Of the feet, 281 bones were studied and 17 found to have stress fractures. The stress fractures in the foot bones "were distributed to the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Proximal_and_distal" title="Anatomical terms of location">proximal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phalanges" title="Phalanges">phalanges</a>" and occurred across all three weight-bearing toes in "statistically indistinguishable" numbers. Since the lower end of the third metatarsal would have contacted the ground first while an allosaur was running it would have borne the most stress. If the allosaurs' stress fractures were caused by damage accumulating while walking or running this bone should have experience more stress fractures than the others. The lack of such a bias in the examined <i>Allosaurus</i> fossils indicates an origin for the stress fractures from a source other than running. The authors conclude that these fractures occurred during interaction with prey, like an allosaur trying to hold struggling prey with its feet. The abundance of stress fractures and avulsion injuries in <i>Allosaurus</i> provide evidence for "very active" predation-based rather than scavenging diets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rothschild-dino_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-rothschild-dino-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>The left <a href="/wiki/Scapula" title="Scapula">scapula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a> of an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allosaurus_fragilis" title="Allosaurus fragilis">Allosaurus fragilis</a></i> specimen catalogued as USNM 4734 are both pathological, both probably due to healed fractures. The specimen USNM 8367 preserved several pathological gastralia which preserve evidence of healed fractures near their middle. Some of the fractures were poorly healed and "formed pseudoarthroses." A specimen with a fractured rib was recovered from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Quarry</a>. Another specimen had fractured ribs and fused vertebrae near the end of the tail. An apparent subadult male <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allosaurus_fragilis" title="Allosaurus fragilis">Allosaurus fragilis</a></i> was reported to have extensive pathologies, with a total of fourteen separate injuries. The specimen MOR 693 had pathologies on five ribs, the sixth neck vertebra the third eighth and thirteenth back vertebrae, the second tail vertebra and its chevron, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastralia" title="Gastralia">gastralia</a> right scapula, manual phalanx I left <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">ilium</a> metatarsals III and V, the first phalanx of the third toe and the third phalanx of the second. The ilium had "a large hole... caused by a blow from above".The near end of the first phalanx of the third toe was afflicted by an <a href="/wiki/Involucrum" title="Involucrum">involucrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-molnar-pathology_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-molnar-pathology-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="5194" height="82" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg/220px-Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg/330px-Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg/440px-Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allosaurus_%22Big_Al_II%22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skeletal restoration of "Big Al II" showing bones with pathologies</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Other pathologies reported in <i>Allosaurus</i> include: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Willow_breaks" title="Willow breaks">Willow breaks</a> in two ribs. Healed fractures in the <a href="/wiki/Humerus" title="Humerus">humerus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radius_(bone)" title="Radius (bone)">radius</a>. Distortion of <a href="/wiki/Joint" title="Joint">joint</a> surfaces in the foot possibly due to <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a> or developmental issues. <a href="/wiki/Osteopetrosis" title="Osteopetrosis">Osteopetrosis</a> along the endosteal surface of a <a href="/wiki/Tibia" title="Tibia">tibia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chin09_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chin09-84">[84]</a></sup> Distortions of the joint surfaces of the tail vertebrae possibly due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osetoarthritis" title="Osetoarthritis">osetoarthritis</a> or developmental issues. "[E]xtensive '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoplastic" title="Neoplastic">neoplastic</a>' <a href="/wiki/Ankylosis" title="Ankylosis">ankylosis</a> of caudals," possibly due to physical trauma as well as the fusion of chevrons to centra. Coossification of vertebral centra near the end of the tail. <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">Amputation</a> of a chevron and foot bone, both possibly a result of bites. "[E]xtensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exostoses" title="Exostoses">exostoses</a>" in the first phalanx of the third toe. Lesions similar to those caused by <a href="/wiki/Osteomyelitis" title="Osteomyelitis">osteomyelitis</a> in two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scapulae" title="Scapulae">scapulae</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bone_spurs" title="Bone spurs">Bone spurs</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Premaxilla" title="Premaxilla">premaxilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ungual" title="Ungual">ungual</a>, and two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metacarpals" title="Metacarpals">metacarpals</a>. Exostosis in a pedal phalanx possibly attributable to an infectious disease. A metacarpal with a round depressed fracture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-molnar-pathology_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-molnar-pathology-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoecology">Paleoecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Paleoecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3413" data-file-width="4061" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg/220px-Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg/330px-Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg/440px-Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barosaurus_lentus1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restoration of <i><a href="/wiki/Barosaurus" title="Barosaurus">Barosaurus</a></i> rearing to defend itself against a pair of <i>Allosaurus</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> was the most common large theropod in the vast tract of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_West" title="American West">Western American</a> fossil-bearing rock known as the <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a>, accounting for 70 to 75% of theropod specimens,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JF07_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-JF07-8">[8]</a></sup> and as such was at the top <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic level</a> of the Morrison <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food web</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JRF03a_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-JRF03a-106">[106]</a></sup> The Morrison Formation is interpreted as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiarid" title="Semiarid">semiarid</a> environment with distinct <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry seasons</a>, and flat <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR89_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAR89-107">[107]</a></sup> Vegetation varied from <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a>-lining forests of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_fern" title="Tree fern">tree ferns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">ferns</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gallery_forest" title="Gallery forest">gallery forests</a>), to fern <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannas</a> with occasional trees such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Araucaria" title="Araucaria">Araucaria</a></i>-like conifer <i><a href="/wiki/Brachyphyllum" title="Brachyphyllum">Brachyphyllum</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KC06_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-KC06-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Morrison Formation has been a rich fossil hunting ground. The flora of the period has been revealed by fossils of <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">green algae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Equisetum" title="Equisetum">horsetails</a>, ferns, <a href="/wiki/Cycad" title="Cycad">cycads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ginkgo" title="Ginkgo">ginkgoes</a>, and several families of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a>. Animal fossils discovered include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snails</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actinopterygii" title="Actinopterygii">ray-finned fishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salamander" title="Salamander">salamanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphenodontia" title="Sphenodontia">sphenodonts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>, terrestrial and aquatic <a href="/wiki/Crocodylomorpha" title="Crocodylomorpha">crocodylomorphans</a>, several species of <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">pterosaur</a>, numerous dinosaur species, and early <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Docodonta" title="Docodonta">docodonts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multituberculata" title="Multituberculata">multituberculates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symmetrodonta" title="Symmetrodonta">symmetrodonts</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triconodonta" title="Triconodonta">triconodonts</a>. Dinosaurs known from the Morrison include the theropods <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratosaurus" title="Ceratosaurus">Ceratosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ornitholestes" title="Ornitholestes">Ornitholestes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tanycolagreus" title="Tanycolagreus">Tanycolagreus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Torvosaurus" title="Torvosaurus">Torvosaurus</a></i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropod" title="Sauropod">sauropods</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Apatosaurus" title="Apatosaurus">Apatosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Brachiosaurus" title="Brachiosaurus">Brachiosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Brontosaurus" title="Brontosaurus">Brontosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Camarasaurus" title="Camarasaurus">Camarasaurus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Diplodocus" title="Diplodocus">Diplodocus</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ornithischia" title="Ornithischia">ornithischians</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Camptosaurus" title="Camptosaurus">Camptosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dryosaurus" title="Dryosaurus">Dryosaurus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Stegosaurus" title="Stegosaurus">Stegosaurus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DJCetal06_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-DJCetal06-109">[109]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> is commonly found at the same sites as <i>Apatosaurus</i>, <i>Camarasaurus</i>, <i>Diplodocus</i>, and <i>Stegosaurus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBBM80_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-DBBM80-110">[110]</a></sup> The Late Jurassic formations of Portugal where <i>Allosaurus</i> is present are interpreted as having been similar to the Morrison but with a stronger <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">marine</a> influence. Many of the dinosaurs of the Morrison Formation are the same genera as those seen in Portuguese rocks (mainly <i>Allosaurus</i>, <i>Ceratosaurus</i>, <i>Torvosaurus</i>, and <i>Stegosaurus</i>), or have a close counterpart (<i>Brachiosaurus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lusotitan" title="Lusotitan">Lusotitan</a></i>, <i>Camptosaurus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Draconyx" title="Draconyx">Draconyx</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OM06_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-OM06-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alloquarrynolang.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="602" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Alloquarrynolang.png/220px-Alloquarrynolang.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Alloquarrynolang.png/330px-Alloquarrynolang.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Alloquarrynolang.png/440px-Alloquarrynolang.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alloquarrynolang.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Locations in the <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">Morrison Formation</a> (yellow) where <i>Allosaurus</i> remains have been found</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> coexisted with fellow large theropods <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratosaurus" title="Ceratosaurus">Ceratosaurus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Torvosaurus" title="Torvosaurus">Torvosaurus</a></i> in both the United States and Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OM06_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-OM06-111">[111]</a></sup> The three appear to have had different <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a>, based on anatomy and the location of fossils. Ceratosaurs and torvosaurs may have preferred to be active around waterways, and had lower, thinner bodies that would have given them an advantage in forest and underbrush terrains, whereas allosaurs were more compact, with longer legs, faster but less maneuverable, and seem to have preferred dry floodplains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BB04_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-BB04-103">[103]</a></sup> <i>Ceratosaurus</i>, better known than <i>Torvosaurus</i>, differed noticeably from <i>Allosaurus</i> in functional anatomy by having a taller, narrower skull with large, broad teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DH98_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-DH98-48">[48]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> was itself a potential food item to other carnivores, as illustrated by an <i>Allosaurus</i> <a href="/wiki/Pubis_(bone)" title="Pubis (bone)">pubic foot</a> marked by the teeth of another theropod, probably <i><a href="/wiki/Ceratosaurus" title="Ceratosaurus">Ceratosaurus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Torvosaurus" title="Torvosaurus">Torvosaurus</a></i>. The location of the bone in the body (along the bottom margin of the torso and partially shielded by the legs), and the fact that it was among the most massive in the skeleton, indicates that the <i>Allosaurus</i> was being scavenged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFJ98_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-CFJ98-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allosaurus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Along with <i><a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">Tyrannosaurus</a></i>, <i>Allosaurus</i> has come to represent the quintessential large, carnivorous dinosaur in western popular culture. It is a common dinosaur in American museums, due in particular to the excavations at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland-Lloyd_Dinosaur_Quarry" title="Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry">Cleveland-Lloyd Dinosaur Quarry</a>; by 1976, as a result of cooperative operations, 38 museums in eight countries on three continents had Cleveland-Lloyd allosaur material or casts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM76_6-17"><a href="#cite_note-JM76-6">[6]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> is the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_fossil" title="State fossil">state fossil</a> of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-statefossil_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-statefossil-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Allosaurus</i> has been depicted in popular culture since the early years of the 20th century. It is top predator in both <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a>'s 1912 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Conan_Doyle_novel)" title="The Lost World (Conan Doyle novel)">The Lost World</a></i>, and its <a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(1925_film)" title="The Lost World (1925 film)">1925 film adaptation</a>, the first full-length motion picture to feature dinosaurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> was used as the starring dinosaur of the 1956 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beast_of_Hollow_Mountain" title="The Beast of Hollow Mountain">The Beast of Hollow Mountain</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-115">[115]</a></sup> and the 1969 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Valley_of_Gwangi" title="The Valley of Gwangi">The Valley of Gwangi</a></i>, two <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a> combinations of living dinosaurs with <a href="/wiki/Western_(genre)" title="Western (genre)">Westerns</a>. In <i>The Valley of Gwangi</i>, Gwangi is billed as an <i>Allosaurus</i>, although <a href="/wiki/Ray_Harryhausen" title="Ray Harryhausen">Ray Harryhausen</a> based his model for the creature on Charles R. Knight's depiction of a <i>Tyrannosaurus</i>. Harryhausen sometimes confuses the two, stating in a DVD interview "They're both meat eaters, they're both tyrants... one was just a bit larger than the other."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> appeared in the second episode of the 1999 <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> television series <i><a href="/wiki/Walking_with_Dinosaurs" title="Walking with Dinosaurs">Walking with Dinosaurs</a></i> and the follow-up special <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ballad_of_Big_Al" title="The Ballad of Big Al">The Ballad of Big Al</a></i>, which speculated on the life of the "Big Al" specimen, based on scientific evidence from the numerous injuries and pathologies in its skeleton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2001_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2001-117">[117]</a></sup> <i>Allosaurus</i> also made an appearance in the Discovery Channel series <i><a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_Revolution" title="Dinosaur Revolution">Dinosaur Revolution</a></i>. Its depiction in this series was based upon a specimen with a smashed lower jaw that was uncovered by paleontologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_R._Holtz,_Jr." title="Thomas R. Holtz, Jr.">Thomas Holtz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Allosaurus also appears in the 2011 BBC docu-mini-series <i><a href="/wiki/Planet_Dinosaur" title="Planet Dinosaur">Planet Dinosaur</a></i> in the episode "Fight for Life".</p>
